  3. [x] **Slice 3 — bench harness client shipped 2026-05-27.** Standalone Rust crate `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/` (tokio + tokio-rustls/ring + serde_json; ~560 LoC). **Judgment call resolved → Rust, not Kāra**: the harness must *open* `wss://` connections, but the v1 Kāra WebSocket stdlib is server-side only (`accept` / `accept_tls` + `recv_*` / `send_*`; no client `connect`/dial), so a kara client would need a whole new client-side TLS-connect + WS-handshake stdlib surface — exactly the "kara client impractical at v1 scale" escape this entry anticipated. A Rust client also keeps measurement honest (client-side perf bugs don't contaminate server numbers). Workspace-excluded (root `Cargo.toml` `exclude` + an empty `[workspace]` table in the crate so it self-roots even under the `.claude/worktrees/<name>/` dev path) — mirrors the `examples/parallax/bench/rust/` precedent. **Measures all three axes**: (a) connect-establishment p50/p95/p99/**p99.9** + min/max/mean, computed locally via nearest-rank from the full sample set — so the high percentiles the wrk-based Parallax harness can't reach (`bench_robustness.md` G4) are exact here; (b) steady-state server RSS before-vs-after (Linux `/proc/<pid>/status` VmRSS, macOS `ps -o rss=`) ÷ N = per-connection bytes; (c) P99 cliff under churn (close+reopen a fraction over rounds; `cliff_ratio = reconnect_p99 / initial_p99`). Spawns the demo + reads `BOUND_PORT` (owns lifecycle + PID for RSS) via `--server-bin`, or targets a running server via `--addr` (+ `--server-pid`). Per-connection `--connect-timeout-ms` (default 10s) so a stuck connection is a counted failure, never a hang. Emits one JSON object on stdout (`ok` flag + config + connect/memory/churn blocks), all logs to stderr — slice 7's CI gate is a pure parse-and-threshold. Clean under `cargo build/clippy/fmt` in-crate. **Verified end-to-end at N=1** (connect ~0.8 ms, RSS delta captured, well-formed JSON, exit 0). ⚠️ **Surfaced a hard blocker at N≥2** — see the `Demo 1 flagship-server accept loop wedges under concurrency` P0 entry immediately below: the demo server holds at most **1 concurrent** (or 2 sequential) connection before its accept loop wedges. Slices 1/2 were only ever smoke-tested at a *single* connection, so this harness is the first multi-connection exercise of the server and caught the wedge — the harness itself is correct and complete; it will produce the M1 number unchanged once that bug is fixed. CI integration lives at line 168 (`CI benchmark gate on flagship demo`). Files: `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/{Cargo.toml,.gitignore,src/main.rs,README.md}`, root `Cargo.toml` (exclude). **Unblocks** slice 4 (M1 verification) — gated now on the concurrency blocker, not on harness availability.

  4. [~] **Slice 4 — M1 100K verification (in progress 2026-05-29 — clean 50K baseline recorded; 100K blocked by a newly-surfaced establishment-throughput collapse).** Ran slice 3's harness against slice 2's server on a single unprivileged Linux box (2 cores, 7.76 GiB, no root/sudo, LLVM 18 via Nix). **Full topology + tuning + results ladder + root-cause analysis written to `docs/investigations/demo1_m1_verification.md`** (the durable artifact this slice produces). Headline outcomes: **(1) Clean baseline — 50,000 stable idle wss:// connections**: 50000/50000 established, 0 failed, in 72.8 s; connect p50 86 ms / p99 208 ms / p99.9 263 ms; **7.9 KB/conn server RSS** (linear from 1K up — so 100K ≈ ~790 MB, memory is *not* the limiter); churn 3×5000 reopen, 0 failed, cliff_ratio 6.6. **(2) Harness enhancement shipped — `--source-ips`**: a single loopback source IP is capped at ~28K by `ip_local_port_range` (32768–60999) and slows badly near exhaustion (measured 214 conn/s @25K single-IP vs 925/s @10K), and widening the range needs root this box lacks. Added a `--source-ips a,b,c,…` flag that binds each connection's source across `127.0.0.0/8` (round-robin) — Linux routes the whole `/8` to `lo` so this works unprivileged, each IP gets its own ephemeral-port pool, N IPs ⇒ ~N×28K ceiling *and* keeps establishment fast (30K across 3 IPs ran at 804/s, 0 failed). Validated empirically before use. Files: `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/src/main.rs` (new `--source-ips` parse/validate + per-connection source-bind via `TcpSocket` + `config.source_ips` in JSON), `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/README.md` (flag docs + refreshed status). **(3) The 100K wall — establishment-throughput collapse (NOT yet fixed; filed as its own P0 blocker below per `feedback_blockers_get_own_tracker_entries`).** The run to 100K (5 source IPs, concurrency 64) accepts at a rate that degrades superlinearly with held-connection count — 925/s @10K → 804/s @30K → 686/s @50K → **~6/s @77K** — stalling acceptance near ~77K (100K would take >1 h and the rate keeps falling). The *hold* is sound: ~77K connections held on **5 OS threads** (parked-task model holds — not 1:1 with threads), RSS linear ~7.9 KB/conn, ~2.4 GiB still free, 77K fds stable. So the bottleneck is server-side new-connection establishment rate, not memory / threads / the client (concurrency was only 64 in-flight). Ruled out by inspection: fd table is `HashMap` (O(1)), `run_once` iterates only ready events (O(ready)). Leading suspect: the accept loop runs the rustls handshake **synchronously inline** plus a per-accept cost scaling with held count (async-sched slices 4/5 not done). **Toolchain note for any re-run:** Nix env — `karac --features llvm` and `karac build` both need `LIBRARY_PATH=/usr/lib RUSTFLAGS="-L native=/usr/lib"` or the final link fails on `-lffi`. **What this slice does NOT yet do:** flip line 165's `M1 — 100K` to `[x]` — that stays open until the establishment-throughput blocker below is fixed and an actual 100K run lands (which, for a *true* public number, still wants a >2-core, root-tunable, multi-client-source rig per the M3 plan; the single-box 50K here is the v1 progress artifact).

  - [x] **P0 BLOCKER — Demo 1 flagship-server accept loop wedges under concurrency (async-scheduler integration). RESOLVED 2026-05-28.** **Demo now establishes + holds connections cleanly up to the mac's loopback ceiling** (`-n 100` 15/15, `-n 500` 3/3, `-n 1000` 2/2, `-n 2000` ok, and **`-n 10000` → 10000/10000 established, 0 failed, p99=46 ms** at `--concurrency 128` matching `kern.ipc.somaxconn`; macOS M5 Pro). Per-connection cost ≈ **8.5 KiB RSS** (so 100K ≈ ~850 MiB — memory-feasible). The ceiling on this mac is *client-side*, not the server: loopback dialing `127.0.0.1 → 127.0.0.1:<port>` is bounded by the ephemeral-port range (`net.inet.ip.portrange` 49152–65535 ≈ 16K), and bursting more concurrent dials than `somaxconn` (=128) drops SYNs — both OS/tuning limits, not server wedges. **A true 100K run still requires a Linux box with multiple client sources + tuned sysctls (it is NOT testable on a single mac over loopback)** — that run lives under Demo 1 slice 4 (line 182). Two independent root causes, both fixed — see the **RESOLUTION** block after the sub-slices. **Surfaced 2026-05-27 by Demo 1 slice 3's bench harness** — the first multi-connection exercise of the `ws_idle_holder` server (slices 1/2 were only ever smoke-tested at a single connection via `nc` / one Python `ssl` client). **Symptom (reproducible, ground truth):** the server accepts + completes the TLS handshake + WS upgrade for the FIRST connection, but its accept loop then wedges once a per-connection handler is parked in `recv_text`. Measured ceilings: **1 connection held concurrently** (open A + hold → B's TLS handshake hangs), or **2 sequential** (open/upgrade/close A, then B succeeds, then C hangs). The bench harness reports `established 1/5` with the rest `connect timed out`. Hard blocker for M1 (line 165) / slices 4–6 — the whole premise is N×100K *concurrent* idle connections. **Repro:** `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/target/release/ws-idle-holder-bench --server-bin <demo> -n 5 --churn-rounds 0 --connect-timeout-ms 3000` → `established 1/5`.

    **ROOT CAUSE — CONFIRMED 2026-05-27 by reading the codegen + runtime (the earlier worker-saturation hypothesis was WRONG — the per-`feedback_reproduce_before_trusting_bug_diagnosis` reproduction paid off).** It is **entirely a codegen issue; the runtime scheduler is already correct.** Each IO method (`accept`/`accept_tls`/`recv_text`/…) lowers to an *inline blocking spin loop* emitted into the calling function's body (`src/codegen/tcp.rs::emit_state_machine_invocation_for_park_on_fd`, mirrored at `src/codegen/call_dispatch.rs:294+` / `method_call.rs` / `mono.rs`): it drives the leaf `karac_park_on_fd` poll-fn on the *calling* thread, whose `state_1` calls `karac_runtime_event_loop_take_wakeups(buf, 1, -1)`, **discards the buffer, and returns `Ready` unconditionally** (`src/codegen/declarations.rs:1500–1516`, explicitly commented "v1 single-task model … any wakeup is ours. Multi-task v2+ extensions will route the buffer through a dispatcher that matches the wakeup's parked pointer"). `take_wakeups` (`runtime/src/event_loop.rs:744`) is an *unfiltered global-queue pop*. So with ≥2 concurrently-parked tasks — the accept loop parked on the listener fd (on `main`'s thread) + a handler parked on its conn fd (on a worker) — both block on the same queue/condvar; a single wakeup is consumed by whichever thread wins and that task returns `Ready` regardless of whether *its* fd fired. The parked handler steals the accept loop's listener-readiness wakeup → the accept loop never resumes → **wedge at exactly 1** (sequential reaches 2 because the handler closes before the next, briefly clearing the contention). The `SchedulerDispatcher` (`event_loop.rs:2318+`) that *does* route wakeups correctly by the `parked` pointer **already exists and is tested, but codegen never starts it or routes through it** — the call path blocks on the global `take_wakeups` instead of yielding to it.

    **FIX — stackless dispatcher-yield async integration** (the deferred "v2+/slice 8e" work). The runtime bones are right (`register_fd(fd, dir, parked_ptr)` + background poller + `SchedulerDispatcher` resuming a task by calling `poll_fn` on the exact `parked` pointer whose fd fired). What's missing is codegen that *yields* to the dispatcher instead of blocking. Sub-slices:

    1. [x] **Async-sched slice 1 — runtime foundation lock-in (2026-05-27).** Confirmed (new test `dispatcher_drives_two_concurrent_parked_tasks_to_completion` in `runtime/src/event_loop.rs`) that the dispatcher drives MULTIPLE concurrently-parked tasks to completion, routing each fd wakeup to the correct task by its `parked` pointer with no cross-task mis-routing — the exact invariant the demo needs and the codegen's blocking-spin model violates. Locks in that the runtime side is correct and the entire remaining fix is codegen. `cargo test -p karac-runtime dispatcher` green (6 tests).
    2. [x] **Async-sched slices 2+3 — dispatcher-yield leaf park (shipped 2026-05-28; merged).** Slices 2 and 3 proved *inseparable* — the executing `tests/park_and_wake.rs` requires the binary to actually *block* until readiness, so the leaf-park body change could not land without the matching caller change. Shipped together: a per-park `KaracParkSlot` condvar primitive (`runtime/src/event_loop.rs`: `karac_runtime_park_slot_{new,wait,signal,free}`); the leaf `karac_park_on_fd` `state_0` allocates the slot + registers the fd + returns `Pending`, `state_1` (run by the dispatcher on real readiness, routed by `parked` pointer) signals the slot + returns `Ready` — **no more global `take_wakeups` block, so no wakeup-stealing**; the caller intercept (`tcp.rs` + a direct-call branch in `call_dispatch.rs`) polls once then blocks on the slot, deregistering the fd afterwards; entry bootstrap starts the dispatcher. **Thread-bound** (a parked task still holds its thread — true thread-release is slices 4/5), but the routing is correct. Three follow-on runtime fixes were required to make it solid under load — see **RESOLUTION** below.
    3. [x] *(folded into slice 2 above — `Pending` propagation for the leaf-park caller landed with the merged slice; the deeper whole-function propagation is slice 4).*
    4. [x] **Async-sched slice 4 — whole-function state-machine transform. DONE via the A2 LLVM-coroutine transform** — LLVM CoroSplit generates the state machine, preserving loops/matches/locals across suspension by construction (spike `docs/spikes/network-async-coroutine-transform.md` §7 slices 2b.3/3). User functions that contain IO (`handle_connection`, `main`) become resumable `poll_fn` state machines — loops / matches / locals preserved across suspension. The heavy slice; this is async/await codegen for Kāra. **NOT needed for the M1 idle-hold metric** (see RESOLUTION — establishment is `main`'s synchronous work and idle held connections don't each need a *running* handler thread); matters for *active* (recv-heavy) connection scale.
    5. [~] **Async-sched slice 5 — `spawn` dispatcher integration. 5a + 5b + 5c DONE** (non-blocking `karac_runtime_spawn_coro` — the worker ramps + returns, `join` waits on the coroutine's completion slot; cooperative cancellation live — `TaskGroup.cancel()` tears down spawned coroutine handlers incl. idle-parked ones via per-task cancel routing + the dispatcher cancel-sweep, 2026-06-06, see the Open work front detailed entry). **Slice 6 (EC2 per-conn density re-measure — user-run) remains.** `spawn` / `TaskGroup.spawn` register the handler's `poll_fn` with the dispatcher; the worker pool (`runtime/src/lib.rs`) releases its thread on `Pending` instead of blocking — so 100K *active* parked handlers cost ≈0 threads. Same caveat as slice 4: not on the idle-hold critical path.

       **Cooperative cancellation breakdown (A2 slice 5b + 5c trigger) — SHIPPED 2026-06-06.** The design below is realized; what landed is summarized in the Open work front detailed entry (per-task cancel routing on `FdState.cancel` via `register_fd_cancel`; dispatcher cancel-sweep `collect_cancelled`/`sweep_cancelled` driven by `taskgroup_cancel` → `request_cancel_sweep`; register-time park-vs-cancel race guard; RAII-on-cancel with user-`defer` carved). The original gap (the dispatcher passed a process-global never-cancelled flag `(task.poll_fn)(task.state, &disp.cancel)`, so no real flag reached a parked coroutine) is closed: the dispatcher now hands each `poll_fn` the registration's own `cancel`. The teardown *mechanism* shipped inert first (`4186c3ac`): the resume shim `__kara_coro_resume(handle, cancel)` checks the flag and, on set, runs `coro.destroy` → the per-park `kara.coro.destroy.N` edge (deregister fd + slice-4 heap drops + `park_slot_signal` + `coro.free`). Ordered sub-slices, as designed (the spike §6⅞ design, verified against current code; "do with review" — concurrency-sensitive):
       - [x] **5b-1 — `TaskGroup.cancel()` surface + flag plumbing (landed inert 2026-06-06).** New runtime `karac_runtime_taskgroup_cancel(group)` (`scheduler.rs`) flips every registered child's `KaracTaskHandle.cancel` under the children lock; `TaskGroup.cancel()` (`runtime/stdlib/task_group.kara`, `ref self`) lowers to it via `method_call.rs` → `lower_taskgroup_cancel` (`task_group.rs`); FFI declared in `codegen.rs`, kept in `lib.rs` + `SYMBOL_KEEP_LIST.md`. Verified both spawn paths register children with the group (`task_group.rs:470`), so the flip reaches coroutine-spawned children too. **Inert** — nothing reads the flag yet (5c-1). Gates: `scheduler.rs::taskgroup_cancel_flips_all_child_cancel_flags` + `_null_and_empty_are_no_ops`; `tests/codegen.rs::test_taskgroup_cancel_lowers_to_runtime_call`.
       - [x] **5c-1 — per-task cancel routing into the dispatcher** (the core wiring; shipped 2026-06-06 in `f5917d63`). **Shipped via a cleaner design than originally specced:** the cancel flag lives on the event-loop *registration* (`FdState.cancel`, bound by `karac_runtime_event_loop_register_fd_cancel` from the coroutine's slot via `karac_runtime_park_slot_cancel_ptr`), **not** by growing the parked record `{poll_fn, state, token}` → `{…, cancel, …}` / `KaracParkedTask`. That leaves the parked-record ABI unchanged across the coroutine and degenerate state-machine paths (no layout reorder, no misread-token hazard). The dispatcher hands each `poll_fn` the registration's own `cancel` (null → the global never-cancelled fallback, preserving non-coro `poll_fn` paths) instead of always `&disp.cancel`; `spawn_coro` binds `slot.cancel = &handle.cancel`.
       - [x] **5c-2 — dispatcher cancel-sweep for idle parked coroutines** (shipped 2026-06-06 in `f5917d63`). `taskgroup_cancel` flips the child flags then calls `karac_runtime_request_cancel_sweep` (per-shard `sweep_requested` flag + waker); each dispatcher, after draining wakeups, `swap(false)`s its flag (clear-before-snapshot) and runs `EventLoop::sweep_cancelled`. The sweep is a **collect-then-invoke split** to avoid holding the `fds` lock across `poll_fn`: phase A (`collect_cancelled`) snapshots cancelled *tokens* under the lock (reads `FdState.cancel`, no parked deref); phase B claims each via the one-shot `take_registration_with_cancel` (dedups vs. a concurrent normal wakeup / completion) and invokes `poll_fn` with the lock released. A register-time park-vs-cancel race guard (`register_fd_cancel` requests a sweep when registering an already-cancelled fd) closes the spawn-races-cancel window.
       - [x] **5c-3 — live mid-flight-cancel ASAN+leak test** (shipped 2026-06-06 in `f5917d63`). E2E `coroutine_taskgroup_cancel_tears_down_idle_parked_handler` (+ `_under_asan`): a handler parked on an idle `accept` with a `Vec[i64]` live across the park, `tg.cancel()`, asserts the group join wakes (watchdog-timeout = hang) and the heap is freed exactly once under ASAN. **Join-status convention decided as-is:** the joiner wakes through the existing `task_join` path (the cancelled coroutine's destroy edge signals the completion slot) — no distinct `CANCELLED` status variant was added; cancel surfaces as a normal join wake, which the watchdog test relies on. (If a future caller needs to *distinguish* cancelled-vs-completed at the join, that's a separate ABI addition, not part of this slice.)
       - [x] **5c-4 — defer-on-cancel. Shipped 2026-06-06.** Decision: **cancel runs user `defer`** (and non-binding `errdefer`), per design.md § *Panic During Suspend* rule 1 and consistent with the `par {}` cancel path. The destroy edge's `emit_coro_destroy_edge_drops` → renamed `emit_coro_destroy_edge_cleanup`, reimplemented to route through `emit_scope_cleanup_for_error_path` (errdefers phase 1, then drops + defers phase 2) with `branch_cancel_ptr` save/null/restore to suppress compile-time recursion through effectful defer bodies. Binding-form `errdefer(e)`'s `e = Cancelled` payload is the carried cross-cutting gap (same as `par`). Test: `coroutine_taskgroup_cancel_runs_user_defer`.
    6. [~] **Async-sched slice 6 — scale e2e.** Local verification done 2026-05-28: N=100/500/1000/2000 clean, and N=10000 → 10000/10000 (0 failed, p99=46 ms) at `--concurrency 128` on macOS M5 Pro. ~8.5 KiB/conn RSS. The mac caps near 16K (loopback ephemeral ports), so the 100K-on-Linux run (multiple client sources + tuned sysctls) is still pending and lives under Demo 1 slice 4 (line 182).

    **RESOLUTION (2026-05-28) — two independent root causes, both fixed.** The original analysis (codegen wakeup-stealing) was correct but *incomplete*; landing the dispatcher-yield slice exposed the wedge was actually a compound bug. Pinned via macOS `sample <pid>` thread-dumps after reasoning alone repeatedly failed (per `feedback_bench_use_timeout`).
    - **(1) Async-scheduler dispatch (the slice-2/3 surface).** Beyond removing the global `take_wakeups`, three runtime fixes were needed: **(a) one-shot dispatch** — the dispatcher resolves the parked record from the *live* registration map under the `fds` lock and removes the token (`EventLoop::take_registration`), so a duplicate/stale wakeup is skipped and the dispatcher never dereferences a record another thread freed; **(b) wake-on-register** — `register_fd` fires the cross-thread waker so a poller blocked in `run_once(None)` notices a freshly-registered (already-readable) listener instead of missing it; **(c) publish-before-arm ordering** — the leaf `state_0` stores `tag=1` + the slot pointer *before* `register_fd`, since `register_fd` is the point after which the dispatcher can re-invoke the poll-fn on another thread.
    - **(2) `spawn`-move double-drop (a separate, pre-existing codegen bug).** `tg.spawn(|| handle_connection(ws))` captures `ws` *by move*, but the spawn lowering (`src/codegen/task_group.rs`) never suppressed the parent's drop of the moved-out captures — so both `main` and the spawned task ran `WebSocket`'s `Drop`, **double-`close()`ing the connection fd**. That corrupts the fd table (a reused fd is closed out from under another connection) and, on macOS, wedges `close()` itself when the fd is concurrently registered in the kqueue by the task's recv park — the `sample` dumps showed `main` stuck in `karac_runtime_tcp_close → close`. Fixed by calling `suppress_user_drop_for_var` for each moved capture in `lower_spawn_shared`. This is the known move-suppression gap flagged at `runtime/src/event_loop.rs` (the `tcp_close` comment) — the working accept loop just exposed it. (Broader move-suppression for non-`UserDrop` cleanup actions — Vec/String captures — remains the tracked phase-7-codegen.md follow-up.)

    **Regression coverage:** runtime `accept_loop_re_park_drains_burst_connections_without_wedging` + `park_slot_*` unit tests + the existing dispatcher tests (`event_loop.rs`); `park_and_wake` + new `park_twice_same_fd_reregisters` E2E; updated `karac_park_on_fd` codegen IR tests. Full `--features llvm` suite green.

    Per `feedback_no_workarounds_fix_compiler`: the compiler was fixed; the demo's accept-loop shape was left untouched as the test case.

  - [x] **Server-side fix LANDED 2026-05-29 — Demo 1 accept-handshake serialization (synchronous TLS handshake on the single accept thread). Residual ~70K local ceiling is rig-bound, not a server defect.** Surfaced by the slice-4 M1 run: the accept rate degraded with held count (925/s @10K → ~6/s @~77K) and stalled short of 100K. **Diagnosis (ground truth — `docs/investigations/demo1_m1_verification.md`):** CPU-per-accept sampled 10K→85K is **flat at ~0.5 ms** (no O(N) CPU growth) and at the collapse the server is **~0% CPU — idle**, with the accept thread blocked in `wait_woken` **socket reads**. So the accept loop was blocked *reading each peer's TLS/upgrade bytes* — `karac_runtime_ws_accept_tls` did `accept()` + the full rustls handshake + RFC 6455 upgrade **synchronously, inline, one-at-a-time** on the single accept thread, so one slow peer's handshake reads stalled the whole loop (a head-of-line block exploitable slowloris-style) even though the client offered up to 64 concurrent handshakes. (fd table is `HashMap` O(1) and `run_once` is O(ready), so the obvious O(N) structures were *not* it — the serialization + per-handshake read-wait was.) **Fix:** `karac_runtime_ws_accept_tls` keeps the cheap `accept(2)` on the accept thread (drains the backlog non-blocking) but offloads the slow handshake+upgrade to a per-listener pool of worker threads (`KARAC_WS_ACCEPT_THREADS`, default 32), returning ready connections from a completed queue. ABI unchanged → no codegen/stdlib/demo changes (per `feedback_no_workarounds_fix_compiler` the demo source is untouched). Validated: 124 runtime tests pass, N=100 e2e handshakes clean, fmt + clippy clean. `runtime/src/event_loop.rs` (+203/−47). **Post-fix the bottleneck moved off the server:** at the (still ~70K) collapse the server is **0% CPU / fully idle**, all 32 handshake workers blocked in socket reads waiting on the peer; the **co-resident tokio bench client** (2 worker threads on 2 shared cores, ~74K fds / 80K tasks) is the limiter — neither side CPU-bound (box ~80% idle), so it is a **latency-bound ping-pong at scale**, i.e. a property of the single-box measurement rig, not the Kāra server. So the *code* defect this entry named is fixed; the residual local ceiling is the rig. **M1's 100K still requires a separate, well-provisioned client machine** (the M3-plan rig) to find the server's true ceiling — tracked under M1 (line 165) / slice 4 (line 182), now a rig requirement, not a code blocker. Per `feedback_blockers_get_own_tracker_entries`, kept as its own entry. (A larger future server-throughput slice — async whole-function transform, slices 4/5 line 193/194 — would let *active* connections scale too; this fix targets the idle-establishment accept path.)

  5. [ ] **Slice 5 — M2 250K + polish layer 1.** Same demo, 2.5× scale. Timer-wheel implementation, allocator behaviour under churn, fd-set primitives. Flips line 134 `M2 — 250K stable idle connections` to `[x]`. Issues surfaced here also get their own follow-on entries.

  6. [ ] **Slice 6 — M3 1M+ + cross-platform parity.** Linux `io_uring` path for file I/O alongside epoll for sockets; macOS kqueue tuning; Windows IOCP parity. Flips line 135 `M3 — 1M+ stable idle connections, cross-platform parity` to `[x]`. v1 public launch unblocks at this slice.

  - [x] **M3 1M Linux idle-hold leg verified 2026-05-29 — 1M / 1M established, 0 failed, 7.62 GB server RSS, ~7800 B/conn linear from 50K → 1M.** Run rig: `r8g.4xlarge` (Graviton4 / 16 vCPU / 128 GB), Ubuntu 24.04 arm64, on-demand. Bench `--server-bin` self-spawns the demo; 27 loopback source IPs (`127.0.0.2..28`) round-robin past the single-tuple ~28K Linux ephemeral-port cap (`--source-ips`, commit `e37b19c`). Sysctls: `somaxconn` / `tcp_max_syn_backlog` = 65535, `ip_local_port_range` = "15000 65535", `tcp_rmem` / `tcp_wmem` mins lowered to 4 K. `nofile` 1.25 M / process. Raw JSON at `docs/investigations/demo1_m3_verification.json`.

    **Headline numbers:** `established: 1000000`, `failed: 0`; `per_conn_bytes: 7800` (matches 50K = 7836 and 100K = 7818 on the same box — **linear scaling to 1M is empirical, not extrapolation**); `rss_after_kb: 7619448` (~7.62 GB server-side); 1M ramp 852 s (~1170 conns/sec, handshake-CPU-bound at high concurrency); total box RAM used 28 GB / 123 GB — well under the 60–80 GB budget originally projected (rustls held-conn state + tuned kernel buffers came in much leaner than estimated; room for 1.5M–2M on the same box).

    **Connect-latency profile at scale** (`connect`): p50 43 ms, p95 1586 ms, p99 1738 ms, p999 1838 ms. The 5 % tail past 1.5 s is the listen-backlog-overflow signature — `dmesg` captured `TCP: request_sock_TCP: Possible SYN flooding on port 127.0.0.1:36605. Sending cookies.` at ~93 K conns into the ramp. Linux switched to SYN-cookie mode; the cookie path completed the ramp but at the ~1 s SYN-retransmit cost for whichever cohort lost their SYN at queue overflow. Root cause + fix scoped as the next bullet.

    **Churn profile** (3 rounds × 100 K reconnects @ 10 % fraction): reconnect p50 1618 ms, p99 1860 ms; **17 % of reconnects (`reconnect_failed: 50007 / 300000`) exceeded the 30 s `--connect-timeout-ms`** — Linux's SYN-retransmit ladder (1+2+4+8+16 = 31 s) overshoots the deadline whenever cookie mode itself is stressed by the 100 K-burst close+reopen. The bench's `ok: true` reports initial-connect success only, so the churn failures are a separate honest read: **this run proves the idle-hold milestone (1 M held without wedging), not a clean churn verdict at 100 K bursts.** `cliff_ratio: 1.07` is misleadingly innocuous — only because the initial-connect floor itself moved up to 1.74 s (the cliff became the floor on both sides); absolute reconnect latency is ~17× the 100 K run's 53 ms p50.

    **Process-side hang-triage note** (so future debugging doesn't repeat the misread): at ~45 min elapsed the bench *appeared* wedged — all threads in `futex_do_wait` per `/proc/<pid>/wchan`, 0 % CPU on `top -bn2`, RSS stable at 11.58 GB. It was **not** wedged; it was **timer-bound on ~50 K tokio 30-second deadlines** firing on the backlog-starved reconnects, which between firings looks identical to a wedge in any sub-30 s sample window. **`futex_wait` + 0 % CPU + stable RSS over a short window is NOT a wedge signal.** Use `pidstat 5` (interval CPU) or a multi-minute `wchan` poll to distinguish. Added as a triage rule under `feedback_bench_use_timeout`.

    **Does NOT flip line 135** — Linux idle-hold leg is one third of M3. Outstanding: Windows IOCP backend (`runtime/src/event_loop.rs` FFIs are `#[cfg(unix)]` only) and macOS kqueue parity at the 1 M tier (largely demonstrated via mio's kqueue backend at M1 100K scale on the prior mac runs, but not at 1 M). Slice 6's open milestone bullet stays open for those; this sub-entry pins the Linux idle-hold leg as done.

  - [x] **`karac_runtime_tcp_bind` listen-backlog fix LANDED + re-verified at 1 M 2026-05-29 — churn-failure rate dropped from 17 % (50 007 / 300 000) to 0 (0 / 30 000); connect-tail latency unchanged, so the "single root cause" framing in this entry's original wording was half-wrong.** Surfaced by the pre-fix M3 verification (`docs/investigations/demo1_m3_verification_prefix.json`); `dmesg` showed `Possible SYN flooding ... Sending cookies` at ~93 K held conns during the initial ramp. `karac_runtime_tcp_bind` (`runtime/src/event_loop.rs`) used `std::net::TcpListener::bind(addr)`, whose `listen(2)` backlog Rust std hardcodes; Linux caps further at `min(backlog, somaxconn)`. The TLS path (`karac_runtime_tls_listener_bind` at `runtime/src/tls.rs`) delegates to `karac_runtime_tcp_bind`, so both surfaces shared the bottleneck. **Fix:** rewrite `karac_runtime_tcp_bind` to use `socket2::Socket` (added as a direct runtime dep; already transitive via mio) with explicit `listen(65535)`. The bench harness also gained `--churn-batch-cap` (default `10_000`) so churn-fraction 10 % of 1 M no longer produces 100 K bursts — see next bullet. **Re-verification 2026-05-29 (`docs/investigations/demo1_m3_verification.json`, same r8g.4xlarge rig):** `established: 1 000 000 / 0 failed`, **`churn.reconnect_failed: 0`** (vs 50 007 pre-fix), `churn.batch: 10 000` (vs 100 000), `per_conn_bytes: 7799.66` (vs 7800.16 — unchanged, as expected). `dmesg` clean of SYN-flood messages, so the listen-queue overflow is genuinely gone. **What the fix did NOT do:** `connect.p99_ms` is **1856 ms** post-fix vs **1738 ms** pre-fix, and `connect.max_ms` is **2306 ms** vs **2017 ms** — i.e. the connect-phase tail is *unchanged* (drift is within run-to-run noise, not a regression). Conclusion: the listen-backlog overflow was the root cause of the **churn-burst failure rate** but **not** of the connect-phase tail latency, contrary to the hypothesis logged in the pre-fix verification entry above. The connect tail is downstream — TLS handshake CPU or accept-loop serialization at concurrency 512. Scoped as a new follow-on entry below. M3 milestone gate (capacity + correctness under churn) is now genuinely satisfied; the residual ~1.8 s connect p99 at 1 M is honest but does not gate the milestone.

  - [x] **Bench `--churn-batch-cap` shipped with the listen-backlog fix 2026-05-29 — option (a) of the prior bullet's original wording, default `10_000`.** Confirmed active in the re-verification run (`churn.batch: 10000` even though `--churn-fraction` defaulted to 0.1 against `N = 1_000_000`). `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/src/main.rs` (CLI + clamp), `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/README.md` (convention note). README documents the cap so high-N users still get cliff-detection signal at small N without overstressing the listener at 1 M.

  - [x] **M3 connect-phase tail latency at 1 M — root-caused 2026-05-30 as bench-rig oversubscription, not a server defect. Headline reporting changed to a concurrency sweep + runtime worker default scales with `num_cpus`. EC2 re-verification done 2026-05-30 (c=64 single point; see next bullet).** The listen-backlog fix above resolved churn-burst failures but did NOT move the connect tail. Diagnosis: the observed mean `466 ms` at `--concurrency 512` matches the queue-math `(concurrency 512 / workers 32) × T_handshake 29 ms = 464 ms` within 0.5 % — i.e. the bench client was 16× oversubscribed for a 32-worker pool on 16 vCPU r8g.4xlarge, and the "tail" is just the equilibrium queue depth, not a wedge. No code path was malfunctioning. **What landed (2026-05-30):** (a) `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/README.md` documents the convention to sweep `--concurrency` across `{64, 128, 256, 512, 1024}` and report the curve as the headline rather than a single oversubscribed point — single-point "1 M @ 512" remains valid as a capacity stress-test, but is not the latency headline; (b) `KARAC_WS_ACCEPT_THREADS` default now scales with host CPUs (`max(32, 2 × num_cpus())` via `std::thread::available_parallelism()` — no extra dep) so a 32 / 48 / 64 vCPU production box gets a proportionally sized worker pool instead of the historic hardcoded 32; (c) `KARAC_WS_STATS=1` env-gated handshake-pool instrumentation (work-queue depth, completion counts, mean handshake µs) so the queue-math is empirically inspectable without bench-harness changes — this is what let us localise the diagnosis. **History footnote:** the stats code + this entry + the two follow-ons below were briefly rolled back by `04f0745` (a "comment cleanup" commit whose body misrepresented its actual diff) and restored in the EC2-re-verification commit; if the diff stat for `runtime/src/event_loop.rs` ever looks suspiciously large again for a "chore" / "cleanup" commit, audit before merging.

  - [x] **EC2 re-verification of the M3 connect-tail fix — done 2026-05-30 on r8g.4xlarge, c=64 single point.** Spun up a fresh r8g.4xlarge against `HEAD` post-fix and ran one concurrency sweep point at `N = 1_000_000, --concurrency 64, --churn-rounds 0`. Result (`docs/investigations/demo1_m3_curve_c64.json`): **connect.mean_ms = 81.74** (vs `~466` at c=512 baseline — 5.7× drop for an 8× concurrency drop); **connect.p99_ms = 256** (vs `1856` baseline — 7.2× drop, larger than the concurrency ratio because queue-length variance compresses too, the predicted Little's-law signature); **connect.p50_ms = 41.0**; **connect.max_ms = 570.7** (vs `2306`); **per_conn_bytes = 7842** (vs `7800` — flat, as expected; the connect-tail fix is a *latency* shape change, not a memory-shape change); 1 000 000 established, 0 failed. **Math check:** the c=64 run reported `1 000 000 / 1277 s ≈ 783 conns/sec` throughput, which against the unchanged 32-worker pool gives effective `T_handshake = 32 / 783 = 40.87 ms`. Predicted mean at c=64 = `(64 / 32) × 40.87 = 81.7 ms`. **Observed: 81.74 ms — match within 0.05 %**, confirming the queue-depth diagnosis empirically as well as analytically. **Open caveat:** `KARAC_WS_STATS=1` produced no server-side dumps on this run because the EC2 binary was built from a checkout where `04f0745` had silently reverted the stats code (see footnote on the entry above); the c=64 client-side numbers are sufficient to verify the diagnosis without server-side stats, but future runs with the restored stats code will give the corroborating server view. **Remaining sweep points (`{128, 256, 512, 1024}`)** are not required for v1 because the c=64 single point already pins the queue-depth shape; if useful for a future README curve, they can be re-run cheaply.

  - [x] **macOS `karac_runtime_tcp_bind` listen-backlog clamp + bench TLS/WS-upgrade bug both LANDED 2026-05-30 — diagnosis hypothesis in this entry's original wording was wrong about which layer was broken.** Two macOS-only issues fell out of the connect-tail investigation; the listen-backlog clamp shipped earlier in the day; the WS-upgrade bug landed in this commit. **(1) `listen(>16384)` silently breaks loopback acceptance on Darwin even with `kern.ipc.somaxconn` raised** — listener stays in `LISTEN` state but new SYNs never get SYN-ACK'd, so `connect()` hangs until the client times out. Confirmed with a plain-Python listener sweep: `listen(16384)` accepts, `listen(32768)` doesn't, regardless of `somaxconn`. **Patched** at `runtime/src/event_loop.rs`: `KARAC_RUNTIME_TCP_LISTEN_BACKLOG` is now `#[cfg(target_os = "macos")]` `16384` and `#[cfg(not(...))]` `65535`. Linux behaviour is unchanged. **(2) bench harness's TLS + WS upgrade handshake failed on every connection on macOS (M5 Pro)** post-clamp — server-side stats showed `failed_total = N` for every conn, but `openssl s_client` to the same demo binary completed a clean TLS 1.3 handshake. The original hypothesis (TLS itself works, something WS-upgrade-specific) was wrong about the *layer* but right about the *step*. **Actual root cause:** the handshake-pool initializer `ws_handshake_pool_for` sets the listener non-blocking so the FFI's accept-drain loop returns `WouldBlock` on an empty backlog. On **BSD-derived kernels** (macOS, FreeBSD, ...) `accept(2)` returns sockets that **inherit `O_NONBLOCK`** from the listener; on **Linux** the accepted socket is **always blocking** regardless of the listener's flags. So the handshake worker's first `TlsConnIo::read` against the accepted (and inherited-non-blocking) socket returned `WouldBlock` before the peer's request arrived, `ws_read_http_request` mapped that to a hard IO error, and every connection died at the WS-upgrade step (mean ~0.5 ms — far too fast to be a real failure). Linux's `accept(2)` semantics made the same code work there. **Fix:** explicit `sock.set_nonblocking(false)` on every accepted socket inside `karac_runtime_ws_accept_tls`'s accept-drain loop, before submission to the work queue — one line, no platform cfg (no-op on Linux where the socket was already blocking). **Diagnosis path** (followed the (a) step from the original entry but the answer was upstream of the WS-upgrade code, not inside it): added per-step `HandshakeStats` counters (`fail_tls_config` / `fail_tls_handshake` / `fail_session_lookup` / `fail_ws_upgrade`) + a `first_errors: Vec<String>` ring drained by the reporter every tick. Bench at N=20, c=4 immediately showed `fail_ws_upgrade=20, fail_tls_handshake=0`, then refining `ws_drive_upgrade_handshake` to return `Result<(), String>` with the failure sub-reason pinpointed `ws_read_http_request: IO error or EOF before complete request` as the literal symptom — i.e. read failure, not parse failure. Steps (b) (compare Linux-vs-macOS upgrade bytes) and (c) (cert trust check) were not needed once the step-level counter pinpointed `WsUpgrade` and the literal error said "EOF before complete request". **Regression test:** `runtime/src/tls.rs::tests::ws_accept_tls_succeeds_with_nonblocking_listener` runs a real client TLS handshake + RFC 6455 upgrade through `karac_runtime_ws_accept_tls` and asserts a non-negative fd; with the `set_nonblocking(false)` line removed it fails with the same `peer closed without close_notify` signature the bench produced. **Verified on M5 Pro:** `KARAC_WS_STATS=1` bench at `N=1000, c=64` reports `established 1000/1000, 0 failed, fail_ws_upgrade=0`. **Unblocks** macOS-at-1M verification (slice 6 line 221 outstanding leg); does not affect the Linux M3 ship which was already verified at 1M.

    **Diagnosis lesson:** the original tracker entry called this "the open TLS/WS-upgrade bug" and proposed three diagnosis steps (a, b, c). Step (a) — per-step counters — was load-bearing; steps (b) and (c) anchored on the WS-upgrade *contents* / the cert layer, but the actual bug was upstream of the WS-upgrade code (socket mode at `accept`). Once the per-step counter pinned WsUpgrade *and* the literal error said "EOF before complete request", the next move was to examine the layer *below* (the transport state of the socket the worker just received) before instrumenting further inside the failing step. The "openssl s_client works while bench fails" observation in the original entry was misleading: `openssl s_client` only exercised the TLS handshake (which works on a non-blocking socket because rustls's `complete_io` retries on WouldBlock internally) — it never attempted a WS upgrade, so the actual bug was never observed via that probe. Per `feedback_reproduce_before_trusting_bug_diagnosis`: the "TLS works, something WS-upgrade-specific" framing was a hypothesis, not a confirmed observation, and locked-in attention on the wrong layer.

  - [x] **Rust comparator (`examples/ws_idle_holder/rust/`) shipped 2026-05-30 — tokio + rustls + tokio-tungstenite mirror of the Kāra demo, benched through the shared `../bench/` harness.** Same end-to-end shape (TLS listener on 127.0.0.1:0, `BOUND_PORT=<n>` stdout convention, accept WS-over-TLS in a loop, hold idle). Same release posture (`lto = "fat"`, `codegen-units = 1`, `strip = "symbols"`, `panic = "abort"`) so the Rust-vs-Kāra delta isn't distorted by a laxer comparator profile. Same listen-backlog values (`socket2` `listen(65535)` on Linux, `listen(16384)` on macOS) so the comparison isn't gated by a smaller queue on one side. Cert + key shared via `include_str!` of `tests/fixtures/tls/{cert,key}.pem` (the same fixtures the Kāra demo inlines as PEM literals). **Initial macOS M5 Pro side-by-side at `N=5000, --concurrency 128, --churn-rounds 0`:** `connect.mean_ms` 4.30 / 4.30 (Kāra / Rust, indistinguishable at the median — both bound by ring's symmetric crypto); `connect.p50_ms` 4.07 / 4.02; `connect.p95_ms` 5.89 / 5.03 (Rust fractionally tighter); **`connect.p99_ms` 7.14 / 13.78** (Kāra ~2× tighter at the tail — tokio's task-per-conn scheduler shows tail variance the synchronous handshake-worker pool avoids); **`per_conn_bytes` 8703 / 30435 (Kāra ~3.5× more memory-efficient — tokio-tungstenite per-task buffer overhead dominates the delta)**. **Caveat:** at N≥10000 c=128 on macOS with the default single-source-IP loopback, the Rust comparator stalls client-side with `Can't assign requested address` (ephemeral-port pressure on the loopback tuple, not a server defect) after ~2000 conns; Kāra completes the same N because its server-side accept-loop processes faster than the client opens, keeping the in-flight ephemeral-port population lower. Use `--source-ips` for N beyond ~5000 on macOS. **Headline comparison ships against r8g.4xlarge EC2**, not macOS — the 16 vCPU / 65535-backlog / 27-source-IP rig used for the Kāra 1M Linux verification is where both impls have headroom to a fair 1M-class numbers; this macOS run is a shakedown that the comparator builds, runs, and produces sensible-shape numbers. See `examples/ws_idle_holder/rust/README.md` for design rationale + the full results table.

  - [x] **2M ceiling-push bench scaffolding shipped 2026-05-30 — `ec2_setup.sh` bumped to 50 loopback aliases (`127.0.0.2..51`) + nofile 3 M, new `run_2m.sh` sibling of `run_1m.sh` with `-n 2000000` and the expanded `--source-ips` list.** Sized for 50 IPs × ~50 K ports ≈ 2.52 M source tuples — 24 % headroom over 2 M, which absorbs ephemeral-port-range exclusions and any TIME_WAIT churn. `run_1m.sh` is unmodified; it consumes 127.0.0.2..28 (27 IPs) and the extra aliases sit idle on a 1M run. Pushbutton-ready for the next r8g.4xlarge ceiling-push run (next entry). Sizing rationale: at the established Kāra `per_conn_bytes ≈ 7842`, 2 M conns is ≈ 15 GiB server RSS, well under the r8g.4xlarge's 128 GiB; the bottleneck at 2 M is OS-tunable (port + fd limits), not RAM, and the bumps above lift both. Files: `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/scripts/ec2_setup.sh` (loopback alias loop + nofile + commentary), `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/scripts/run_2m.sh` (new, ~90 LoC mirroring run_1m.sh).

  - [x] **M3 1 M Linux re-verification 2026-05-30 post-WS-upgrade-fix — numbers within run-to-run drift of the line 229 baseline; confirms today's `set_nonblocking(false)` fix (line 231) did NOT regress the Linux path.** Same `r8g.4xlarge` rig, same `run_1m.sh` config (`N = 1_000_000, --concurrency 64, --churn-rounds 0`, 27-source-IP loopback fan-out). EC2 output at `kara-1m.json`; headline comparison:

    | metric | line 229 baseline | re-verify | delta |
    |---|---|---|---|
    | established / failed | 1 000 000 / 0 | 1 000 000 / 0 | flat |
    | `connect.mean_ms` | 81.74 | 83.93 | +2.7 % |
    | `connect.p50_ms` | 41.0 | 41.0 | flat |
    | `connect.p99_ms` | 256.0 | 267.4 | +4.4 % |
    | `per_conn_bytes` | 7842 | 7846.6 | +0.06 % |
    | ramp time | 1277 s | 1311 s | +2.7 % |

    All deltas within typical run-to-run noise on the same rig. `per_conn_bytes` essentially flat confirms the WS-upgrade fix is a no-op on Linux (where `accept(2)` already returns blocking sockets — the BSD-`O_NONBLOCK`-inheritance bug at line 231 only fired on macOS, and this re-run is the negative-control on Linux). `dmesg` was permission-denied on the unprivileged shell (apparmor-restricted), but the visible boot tail had no SYN-flood entries and the 0-failed + flat `per_conn_bytes` independently confirm the listen backlog isn't being saturated. **Unblocks** the 2 M ceiling push (next entry).

  - [x] **EC2 Rust comparator at 1 M on r8g.4xlarge — done 2026-05-30. Confirms the 3.55× per-conn-bytes ratio at Linux-1M scale; flips the demo-arc framing from simple "parity" to a multi-dimensional Kāra-wins-memory-and-tail / Rust-wins-throughput-and-mean tradeoff.** Same rig, same `run_1m.sh` config (`N = 1_000_000, --concurrency 64, --churn-rounds 0`, 27-source-IP loopback fan-out). EC2 output at `rust-1m.json`; head-to-head with the line 239 Kāra re-verification:

    | metric | Kāra | Rust | winner |
    |---|---|---|---|
    | established / failed | 1 000 000 / 0 | 1 000 000 / 0 | tie |
    | ramp time | 1311 s | **1004 s** | Rust (−23 %) |
    | `connect.mean_ms` | 83.93 | **64.26** | Rust (−23 %) |
    | `connect.p50_ms` | 41.0 | **2.59** | Rust (−94 %) |
    | `connect.p95_ms` | **222.73** | 250.83 | Kāra (−11 %) |
    | `connect.p99_ms` | **267.39** | 303.94 | Kāra (−12 %) |
    | `connect.p99.9_ms` | **315.41** | 361.58 | Kāra (−13 %) |
    | `connect.max_ms` | **446.59** | 506.04 | Kāra (−12 %) |
    | **`per_conn_bytes`** | **7846** | **27895** | **Kāra (3.55×)** |

    **Queue-math sanity check.** Rust hit `1 M / 1004 s = 996 conns/sec` on a 16-vCPU box. Treating tokio's multi-thread runtime as 16 effective workers, `T_handshake_eff = 16 / 996 = 16.06 ms`; predicted mean at c=64 = `(64 / 16) × 16.06 = 64.24 ms`. **Observed 64.26 ms — match within 0.03 %.** Same shape holds for Kāra at 32 synchronous workers (line 229 math): `32 / 783 = 40.87 ms`; predicted mean = `(64 / 32) × 40.87 = 81.7 ms`. **Observed 83.93 ms — match within 2.7 %.** Both runs are queue-depth-limited; tokio's async I/O effectively gives lower per-handshake cost by overlapping in-flight conns per worker (one async runtime worker carries many tokio tasks), while Kāra's synchronous handshake-worker pool runs one conn per OS thread. The throughput delta isn't a Kāra defect — it's the synchronous-vs-async model tradeoff, which is the same tradeoff that gives Kāra the 3.55× memory advantage (no per-task tokio buffer overhead, no async-state-machine prologue per conn).

    **Demo-arc shift.** The earlier "1 M parity" framing was conversationally inaccurate (the macOS-5K shakedown is not a headline parity claim) AND now empirically nuanced (mean and p50 are *not* equal at scale). Honest frame: at 1 M idle WS-over-TLS on a single r8g.4xlarge, **Kāra and Rust both deliver 1M / 0 failed; Rust wins throughput and mean (~25 %) by overlapping in-flight handshakes async; Kāra wins tail (~12 % at p95→max) by predictable queue depth; Kāra wins memory decisively (3.55×)**. For idle-heavy workloads (chat, IoT push, ISP gateways) where memory is the binding constraint, Kāra's 7.8 KB/conn lets a single 128 GiB box hold ~4 M conns where Rust would OOM at ~3.8 M — that's the strong demo claim. The throughput trade is acceptable when latency is dominated by network propagation (idle-hold doesn't generate steady connect-rate load anyway), and the tighter tail is its own production-stability win. `dmesg` permission-denied as in the Kāra re-verify; the visible boot tail had no SYN-flood entries and 0-failed + flat per_conn_bytes confirm the listen backlog is clean.

  - [x] **`KARAC_WS_ACCEPT_THREADS=256` worker-pool sweep at 1 M c=64 — done 2026-05-30. Hypothesis (8× workers closes the 41 ms p50 gap vs Rust by dissolving the queue depth) falsified empirically. p50 unmoved at 41 ms; mean and tails got *worse* by 10–24 %; ramp +10 %. Conclusion: the default `max(32, 2 × num_cpus())` is already past the diminishing-returns knee on a 16-vCPU box, and the p50 floor is architectural (likely park/wake latency between leaf I/O completion and handshake-worker pickup), not a worker-pool-sizing question.** Run: `KARAC_WS_ACCEPT_THREADS=256 bash run_1m.sh ...` on the same r8g.4xlarge. Output at `kara-1m-w256.json`. Comparison vs the line 240 default-w=32 baseline:

    | metric | w=32 (default) | w=256 | delta |
    |---|---|---|---|
    | established / failed | 1 000 000 / 0 | 1 000 000 / 0 | flat |
    | ramp time | 1311 s | 1442 s | +10 % |
    | `connect.mean_ms` | 83.93 | 92.25 | +10 % |
    | **`connect.p50_ms`** | **41.0** | **41.01** | **±0** |
    | `connect.p95_ms` | 222.73 | 253.72 | +14 % |
    | `connect.p99_ms` | 267.39 | 303.97 | +14 % |
    | `connect.p99.9_ms` | 315.41 | 359.32 | +14 % |
    | `connect.max_ms` | 446.59 | 554.96 | +24 % |
    | `per_conn_bytes` | 7846.6 | 7843.7 | flat |

    **Interpretation.** Two findings. (1) **p50 is not queue-depth-limited.** 8× more workers (32 → 256) leaves p50 unchanged at 41.01 ms vs 41.0. The queue-math identity from line 267 (`predicted_mean = (c / w) × T_handshake_eff`) predicts *mean*, not *p50*: below the queue-depth curve there is a per-connection latency floor that more parallelism cannot reduce. Most likely cause is the park/wake round-trip from leaf I/O completion (epoll readiness) → `ParkSlot` wake → handshake-worker pickup — scheduling granularity, not handshake CPU work. The 16× gap to Rust's p50 = 2.59 ms is consistent with one async-runtime-worker hop vs Kāra's full park→wake→OS-thread-schedule sequence per conn. (2) **Past w=32 on 16 vCPU, throughput decays.** With 256 OS threads on 16 vCPUs the kernel scheduler thrashes; mean and tails degrade roughly in proportion (~10–24 %). So `2 × num_cpus()` is already past the knee on this rig, and the default scaling formula at `runtime/src/event_loop.rs::ws_handshake_thread_count` does not need adjustment. **`per_conn_bytes` flat (7843.7 vs 7846.6)** confirms the held-conn memory shape is independent of worker-pool size — 256 threads × 8 KB OS stack ≈ 2 MB, dwarfed by 7.6 GB held-conn state. The 3.55× memory advantage over Rust (line 253) is unaffected. **Decision.** Do not change the default. The 41 ms p50 vs Rust's 2.59 ms is real but is not in scope of this knob; closing it requires a separate runtime perf slice (next entry). p95 / p99 / per_conn_bytes lead remains the demo claim.

  - [x] **P2 — Runtime perf follow-on: localise the 41 ms p50 floor in the handshake-accept path. ✅ RESOLVED 2026-06-06 — root cause was NOT park/wake; it was a missing `TCP_NODELAY` (Nagle × delayed-ACK).** The standing hypothesis below (each accept pays a `ParkSlot` register/wake/dispatch round-trip) is **superseded — refuted**. A controlled loopback handshake-latency probe (`tls::tests::handshake_latency_probe`, `#[ignore]`d; sequential connects isolate the per-conn floor) on a fresh Graviton box pinned it to Nagle: the TLS handshake + RFC 6455 upgrade is a multi-round-trip exchange of small records, and Kāra — alone in the comparator set — never set `TCP_NODELAY`, so a handshake record sat withheld behind an unacked segment until the peer's ~40 ms delayed-ACK timer fired. (Rust's ~2.59 ms is explained: `tokio` sets `TCP_NODELAY` by default — same kernel/network/client, the only difference was Nagle. The THREADS=256 null result at lines 276/290 is consistent: more workers can't move a floor that isn't queue/scheduling-bound.) **2×2 probe, N=500 sequential, Linux loopback TLS+WS:** server-nodelay OFF/client OFF → p50 **41.93 ms** (reproduces the campaign floor to the ms); server ON → **6.01 ms** (7× collapse); the residual ~47 ms *tail* is client-side Nagle and clears when the client sets it too (both ON → flat ~6 ms). **Fix landed** — `set_nodelay(true)` on the server accept paths (`ws_accept_tls`, `ws_accept`, `tls_accept`) and the client connect path (`tls_client_connect`); commits on `main`. **Authoritative record + full table:** [`bench/REPORT.md` §Connect-p50](../../examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/REPORT.md). **Confirmed at scale 2026-06-06** (250K, c=64, fresh `m8g.4xlarge`; toggled A/B, env-gated server `set_nodelay`): OFF leg reproduces the floor at **p50 45.01 ms** (250K/0), the fix collapses it **27× → 1.63 ms** (250K/0), density untouched (12,126 B/conn). Post-fix Kāra now holds the **fastest connect-p50 in the comparator set** (1.63 vs Rust 2.93 / Go 3.37 / Netty 3.8 / .NET 4.6 / Phoenix 10.7). Bonus finding: the 45 ms stall was also an establishment-throughput throttle — removing it cut the 250K ramp **~19× (210 s → 11 s)**. Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/demo1_p50_nodelay_{250k_off,250k_on,50k_on}.json`. (1M/2M post-fix p50 not re-run — the per-conn fix is scale-invariant; not worth the rig cost.) **Does NOT gate v1 demo** (the demo-arc trades p50 for tail + memory; density is the headline and is unaffected — this is a latency fix orthogonal to per-conn memory). Bidirectional ref: experiment + decision logged in the entry directly above.

  - [x] **M3 2 M ceiling push on r8g.4xlarge — Kāra — landed 2026-05-30.** **⚠️ PROVISIONAL per-conn density — measured pre-line-17 with non-executing handlers (see line 17 sub-bullet 2); 7,860.7 B understates a working server, re-measure after line 17 (expect ~12–13 KB).** 2,000,000 / 2,000,000 established, 0 failed. `per_conn_bytes = 7860.7` — **0.19 % drift vs the 1 M baseline of 7,846 B**, end-to-end empirical confirmation that the density ratio is scale-invariant (the load-bearing assumption behind the 250K-scale-per-comparator decision in `bench/REPORT.md § Scale per comparator`). Server RSS 14.65 GiB held; matches `per_conn_bytes × N` to within rounding. Connect latencies: `mean=214.6ms`, `p50=41.0ms` (matches the architectural floor from the follow-on entry above — not a regression), `p95=673.9ms`, `p99=798.2ms`, `p999=932.6ms`, `max=1204.9ms`; tail expansion vs the 1 M baseline tracks held-conn count. Ramp 6706.86 s (~1 h 51 min) = 298 conns/sec, vs the predicted 783 conns/sec extrapolated from the 1 M ramp — the established superlinear-degradation pattern on connection establishment under increasing held-conn count (epoll fd-set walk, accept-queue contention); orthogonal to per-conn memory which stayed flat. Filed against the active-traffic stress slice (#66) for future localisation; **does not affect the density headline**. Surfaced + closed a tuning-floor gap on the bench rig: the first attempt aborted on `VFS: file-max limit 3000000 reached` mid-ramp (the default `ec2_setup.sh` sysctl block didn't raise `fs.file-max` because 1 M conns fit under the 3 M default but 2 M conns don't); patched to `fs.file-max=8000000` in `examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/scripts/ec2_setup.sh` in the same revision so future 2 M+ runs don't repeat the abort. Sanity-check criteria from the pre-run spec all met (`established == 2M, failed == 0` ✓; `per_conn_bytes` ±5 % of 1 M ✓; `dmesg` clean on the successful run, file-max abort was the *prior* attempt ✓). Run via `bash examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/scripts/run_2m.sh examples/ws_idle_holder/ws_idle_holder kara-2m.json` on r8g.4xlarge after `sudo bash scripts/ec2_setup.sh` + `sudo sysctl -w fs.file-max=8000000` (the latter inline since the script update lands in this same revision). Raw JSON: `kara-2m.json` on the bench rig; mirror to `docs/investigations/demo1_m3_2m.json` queued for the next docs/investigations sync. Companion REPORT.md update lands in the same commit: `Idle-hold @ 2M` row populated, TL;DR Kāra status flipped to `landed @ 2M`, scale-invariance language updated to cite the 2 M endpoint rather than the 1.86 M mid-ramp datapoint, status matrix Kāra row flipped, change-log entry added. **Unblocks** the Rust-2M head-to-head entry directly below (Rust-2M now has a confirmed pair-side ceiling number to head-to-head against, which was the rationale that flipped that entry's open decision to "run" on 2026-05-30; entry text now says "locked: run").

  - [x] **Rust comparator at 2 M on EC2 r8g.4xlarge — landed 2026-05-30.** 2,000,000 / 2,000,000 established, 0 failed. `per_conn_bytes = 27893.0` — **0.33 % drift vs the 1 M baseline of 27,895 B** (matches Kāra's own 0.19 % drift; both impls' per-conn-bytes are empirically scale-invariant). Server RSS held 51.96 GiB (54,481,448 KiB), comfortably under the 128 GiB box. The headline **3.55× density ratio at 2 M is identical to the 1 M ratio** (27,893 / 7,861 = 3.548×), end-to-end empirical confirmation that the [`bench/REPORT.md § Scale per comparator`](../../examples/ws_idle_holder/bench/REPORT.md#scale-per-comparator) scale-invariance argument holds at the ceiling — the credibility-comparator pillar that the commercial 250K-vs-250K comparisons rest on. Connect latencies: `mean=206.9ms`, `p50=2.93ms` (vs Kāra's 41.0 ms — the architectural floor at the line 287 follow-on entry is confirmed Kāra-side, **not** a workload artifact), `p95=745.3ms`, `p99=872.1ms`, `p999=1014.9ms`, `max=1336.4ms`. Ramp 6464.76 s (~108 min) = **309 conns/sec, vs Rust's 996 conns/sec at 1 M** — Rust shows the same superlinear-degradation pattern that Kāra showed (line 289); both impls are queue-depth-limited the same way when held-conn count climbs. Head-to-head with Kāra at the 2 M ceiling:

    | metric | Kāra | Rust | winner |
    |---|---|---|---|
    | established / failed | 2 000 000 / 0 | 2 000 000 / 0 | tie |
    | ramp time | 6707 s | **6465 s** | Rust (−3.6 %) |
    | `connect.mean_ms` | 214.6 | **206.9** | Rust (−3.6 %) |
    | `connect.p50_ms` | 41.0 | **2.93** | Rust (−93 %) |
    | `connect.p95_ms` | **673.9** | 745.3 | Kāra (−10 %) |
    | `connect.p99_ms` | **798.2** | 872.1 | Kāra (−9 %) |
    | `connect.p99.9_ms` | **932.6** | 1014.9 | Kāra (−8 %) |
    | `connect.max_ms` | **1204.9** | 1336.4 | Kāra (−10 %) |
    | **`per_conn_bytes`** | **7,861** | **27,893** | **Kāra (3.55×)** |

    **Demo arc on landing.** "Both impls hold 2 M on the same machine, Kāra at 3.55× less RAM" — strictly stronger than Kāra-solo, and the multi-dimensional tradeoff from the line 253 1 M head-to-head **holds at the ceiling**: Rust wins throughput and mean (~4 %) + p50 (~14× tighter handshake hop); Kāra wins tail (~8–10 % at p95→max) and memory (3.55×, scale-invariant). For idle-heavy workloads (chat, IoT push, ISP gateways) where memory is the binding constraint, Kāra's 7.8 KB/conn means a 128 GiB box holds ~16 M conns where Rust OOMs at ~4.6 M — same 3.55× headroom that holds at 1 M. **Acceptance criteria all met:** `established == 2 M ✓`; `failed == 0 ✓`; `per_conn_bytes ≥ 3.0× Kāra's 7861` (= 23,583 B floor — `27,893 ≥ 23,583` ✓ at 3.55×); `dmesg` clean on the measured run (the visible `VFS: file-max limit 3000000 reached` entry at uptime 8682 s — i.e. *after* the 6465 s ramp completed and the JSON was emitted with `ok: true` + 2 M established + 0 failed — is a teardown-phase touch of the legacy sysctl cap that didn't perturb the measured numbers; recoverable on the next 2 M+ run by the same `fs.file-max=8000000` patch landed alongside the Kāra 2 M run). Raw JSON: `rust-2m.json` on the bench rig (instance terminated post-run); mirror to `docs/investigations/demo1_m3_2m_rust.json` queued for the next docs/investigations sync. Companion REPORT.md update lands in the same commit: TL;DR Rust row + apples-to-apples caveat + Rust per-comparator section + commercial reframe + status matrix all flipped to `landed @ 2M`. Top-level `README.md` `Concurrency Runtime` line updated to lead with the head-to-head 2 M number rather than the Kāra-solo 1 M number. **Closes** the credibility-comparator scale-invariance story; the commercial-comparator rows (Phoenix, Java/Netty, Go, .NET ×2, Node) now run at 250K with the linearity-escalation gate per the published scale-per-comparator decision. Tracked as wip task #63 (now closed).

Scope was Kāra 1M only (x86 2M deliberately **skipped** — the 1M→2M scale-invariance is already locked on arm64 at 0.19 % drift and is a per-conn-allocation property orthogonal to ISA, so x86 2M would re-prove a proven, architecture-independent fact at ~5× the rig cost). Rig: `c7i.8xlarge` (Intel / 32 vCPU / 64 GB), Ubuntu 24.04 x86_64, on-demand; same `run_1m.sh` config (`-n 1000000 --concurrency 64 --churn-rounds 0`, 27 loopback source IPs). dmesg clean. **Surfaced + fixed two karac/rig gaps en route** (the first-ever x86_64-Linux build): (i) karac emitted non-PIC objects, so the demo failed to link against the default-PIE Ubuntu linker (`R_X86_64_32` relocation) — fixed by setting `RelocMode::PIC` in `create_target_machine` (commit `bda38682`); (ii) `ec2_setup.sh` never raised `fs.nr_open` and `limits.d` was overridden by systemd's session `DefaultLimitNOFILE`, capping fds below 1 M — fixed by raising `fs.nr_open` + a systemd drop-in, plus a server-bin path-absolutisation papercut in the run scripts (commit `6437e765`). Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/demo1_m3_1m_x86.json`. Tracked as wip task #62 (now closed). Per `feedback_kata_readme_sweep_on_cross_cutting_fix`, the bench corpus is now two arches — but the x86 leg is a one-shot credibility datapoint, not a routine re-bench target.

  - [x] **Phoenix Channels comparator (`examples/ws_idle_holder/phoenix/`) — the rhetorically critical COMMERCIAL-tier comparator, landed 2026-06-06 (wip task #67).** Minimal Phoenix 1.7.x Channels app (`UserSocket → BenchChannel(room:*) → Presence.track`), Elixir 1.17.3 / OTP 25, Cowboy transport, **in-process** Erlang `:ssl` — the real-world Elixir prod default (Channels + Presence, **not** raw Cowboy). Harness gained `--ws-path` + `--phx-join` (Phoenix v2 join: `phx_join` → `ok` `phx_reply` before a conn counts established — engages the channel + presence layer); run scripts gained `BENCH_EXTRA_ARGS` + `PRESENCE` pass-through (commit `1fbb2cf4`, prep). **At-scale on a fresh 16-vCPU Graviton / 61 GB box, co-located over loopback, `+Q 2000000 +P 8000000`, `--hold-secs 10`. Headline = presence-OFF clean idle hold:** 250K → **250,000 / 0 failed, `per_conn_bytes = 105,267 B (102.8 KiB)`**, connect p50 10.7 / p99 17.9 ms; 50K → 50,000 / 0 failed, 107,204 B/conn. **Linearity drift 50K→250K = −1.8 %** (< 5 % gate → published at 250K, no 1M escalation — despite Phoenix being the flagged escalation candidate; BEAM per-conn proved flat). **Headline: Kāra holds 8.69× the density** (12,114 B vs 105,267 B) — Phoenix is the **heaviest comparator measured**, 2.37× Go (44,386 B) and 3.69× Rust (27,893 B): Erlang `:ssl` (several processes + per-socket buffers per conn) + a transport **and** channel process per conn, none shared. The "Phoenix holds 2M connections" legend was plain `ws://`, no Channels, no Presence, BEAM-tuned — a much lighter config than this real-world default. **Presence-ON is NOT headlined:** `Presence.track`'s `presence_diff` is O(N²) server→client traffic an idle (non-draining) client backs up server-side, so the 50K presence-ON read (192,829 B, +83.6 KiB) is an undrained-backpressure upper bound, not steady-state (prod shards presence topics); 250K presence-ON not run (confounded + ~47 GiB near box ceiling). This **inverts** the wip plan's "presence-ON = headline" assumption (which presumed presence ≈ +2 KB). Connect p50 ~11 ms beats Kāra's ~41 ms architectural floor (same multi-axis tradeoff; density is the headline). Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/phoenix_idle_{250k_nopresence,50k_nopresence,50k_presence}.json`.   - [x] **Java/Netty comparator (`examples/ws_idle_holder/java/`) — largest commercial-TAM comparator, landed 2026-06-06 (wip task #68).** Raw Netty 4.1.115 WS-over-TLS idle holder (`SslHandler → HttpServerCodec → HttpObjectAggregator → WebSocketServerProtocolHandler("/") → echo`), OpenJDK 21.0.11, **in-process** JDK JSSE `SSLEngine` — the high-density Java WS prod default (not Spring/Vert.x/Akka). Bare-WS (upgrade at `/`) → **no harness changes**; run scripts drive it via `JAVA_OPTS` (heap/GC) + `BENCH_EXTRA_ARGS` env. Prep + a `maven-compiler-plugin` pin (apt Maven 3.8.7 defaulted to target 1.5) landed `4a3b2b31` + `b8d6e8b6`. **Key finding: the JVM does NOT fit the harness's `per_conn_bytes = RSS-delta / N` metric** — JVM RSS is dominated by GC heap-commit (`-Xmx`-dependent, far larger than the live set), so per-conn RSS is a **dial**: post-GC **live set ~8.3 KB @ 250K (below Kāra)**, **balanced-heap (`-Xmx4g`) 250K = 14.4 KB/conn (1.19× Kāra)**, `-Xmx24g` over-commit 21.6 KB, ZGC 61.7 KB. **The scale-invariant per-conn is the marginal RSS slope ≈ 12.8 KB (1.06× Kāra)**, flat across every G1 run (50K↔250K, both heaps). **All six runs 250,000 / 50,000 established, 0 failed**; connect p50 3.8 ms (beats Kāra's ~41 ms floor). **Java/Netty is Kāra's CLOSEST density competitor and the second-densest stack measured** — ahead of Rust (27.9 KB), Go (43.4 KB), Phoenix (102.8 KB): JDK JSSE packs SSL state on a poolable GC heap. The 50K→250K RSS-delta/N drift (−32 %) is the fixed JVM/G1 base amortizing, **not** per-conn non-linearity (marginal flat) → no 1M escalation. ZGC trades memory for pause latency (more heap reservation, same live set) — dropped from the headline. Reframe led as "Kāra matches the densest mainstream JVM stack with none of the JVM ops surface (multi-GB fixed heap base + RAM-vs-GC-CPU dial)," not a box-count cut. Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/netty_g1_{250k,50k}_{balanced,xmx24g}.json`, `netty_zgc_250k.json`. REPORT.md §Java (full dial + head-to-head + caveats) / TL;DR row + footnote / both status matrices / hardware row / commercial-reframe / consolidated caveats / top banner all updated; `java/README.md` carries the results + turnkey rig recipe. Authoritative record is `bench/REPORT.md` — this entry is the network-runtime-tracker pointer.
  - [x] **.NET/ASP.NET Core (Linux) comparator (`examples/ws_idle_holder/dotnet/`) — commercial-tier comparator, landed 2026-06-06 (wip task #71).** Raw ASP.NET Core Kestrel + `app.UseWebSockets()` echo middleware (no SignalR), .NET 8.0.421 (`net8.0`), `WebApplication.CreateSlimBuilder`, **in-process** Kestrel HTTPS over **OpenSSL** (the Linux .NET default) — the lean ASP.NET Core WS prod default. Bare-WS (upgrade at `/`) → **no harness changes**; self-contained `linux-arm64` publish → the rig needs no .NET install (prep landed `4c6bf47a`). **At-scale on a fresh 16-vCPU Graviton / 61 GB box, co-located over loopback, Server GC (`ServerGarbageCollection=true`, the Web SDK default):** 250K → **250,000 / 0 failed, `per_conn_bytes = 54,125 B (52.9 KiB)`**, server RSS 12.66 GiB; 50K → 50,000 / 0 failed, 54,869 B/conn, connect p50 4.6 / p99 15.0 ms. **Linearity drift 50K→250K = −1.4 %** (< 5 % gate → published at 250K, no 1M escalation). **Key finding: the OPPOSITE of the JVM — .NET's per-conn RSS is a *real* number, not a GC-heap dial.** Server GC also commits heap lazily, but three independent signatures prove the ~53 KiB is genuinely live: marginal RSS slope (~52.7 KiB) ≈ the absolute (52.9 KiB), the −1.4 % linearity, and a Workstation-GC sidebar (`DOTNET_gcServer=0`) that lands at 53,781 B @ 50K — **within ~2 % of Server GC** (a GC over-commit, as on the JVM, would have collapsed under Workstation GC; it doesn't, because open connections hold the memory live: `SslStream` buffers + Kestrel `System.IO.Pipelines` segments + WS frame state, none pooled). **Headline: Kāra holds 4.47× the density** (12,114 B vs 54,125 B) — **.NET is the second-*heaviest* comparator measured**, between Go (44,386 B) and Phoenix (105,267 B), above Rust (27,893 B) and Netty (14.4 KB balanced). Connect p50 4.6 ms beats Kāra's ~41 ms architectural floor (same multi-axis tradeoff; density is the headline). Reframe = the standard "one tier down → ~50 % infra cost" (16 GiB → 8 GiB `m7g` tier), off a density gap larger than Go/Rust. Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/dotnet_linux_{250k,50k,50k_wks}.json`. Authoritative record is `bench/REPORT.md` — this entry is the network-runtime-tracker pointer.
  - [x] **Node.js (`ws`) comparator (`examples/ws_idle_holder/node/`) — commercial-tier comparator, landed 2026-06-06 (wip task #73).** Raw `ws` 8.21.0 over in-process `https.createServer`/**OpenSSL 3.x** (no socket.io — that's stretch #75), Node 24.15.0 LTS, single-threaded **libuv** event loop, single process (no `cluster`) — the lean Node WS prod default. Bare-WS (upgrade at `/`) → **no harness changes**; `npm ci` vendors `ws` from the committed `package-lock.json` (no self-contained bundle like Go/.NET — Node is an interpreter; prep landed `33704566`). **At-scale on a fresh 16-vCPU Graviton / 61 GB box, co-located over loopback:** 250K → **250,000 / 0 failed, `per_conn_bytes = 41,378 B (40.4 KiB)`**, server RSS ~9.86 GiB, connect p50 50.8 / p99 92.7 ms; 50K → 50,000 / 0 failed, 42,131 B/conn. **Linearity drift 50K→250K = −1.79 %** (< 5 % gate → published at 250K, no 1M escalation; per-conn *falls* with N). **Key finding: like .NET and the OPPOSITE of the JVM — a *real* number, not a GC-heap dial.** V8 has a managed heap (`--max-old-space-size` = `-Xmx` analog), but a heap-cap sidebar (`--max-old-space-size=512` @ 50K → 42,161 B) moved RSS-delta/N by **+0.07 %** — capping old-space barely touched it, so the ~41 KiB is genuinely live native memory (per-conn OpenSSL record buffers + libuv handle state + Node stream buffers + `ws` frame state, all outside the V8 GC heap; a GC over-commit would have collapsed under the cap). **Headline: Kāra holds 3.42× the density** (12,114 B vs 41,378 B) — Node is the **4th-densest comparator measured**, between Rust (27,893 B) and Go (44,386 B): **denser than Go (~7 %)** because the single-threaded event loop pays **no per-conn goroutine/thread stack** (the structural lever Kāra's own reactor pushes 3.42× further), yet **lighter than .NET (1.31×) despite sharing OpenSSL** — isolating a real libuv-vs-Kestrel runtime delta with the TLS substrate held constant. Node's one weak axis: connect p50 ~50 ms (vs Go's ~3 ms), a single-thread handshake-throughput artifact (every TLS handshake serialized through one OpenSSL context), not a density or steady-state cost. Reframe = the standard "one tier down → ~50 % infra cost" (16 GiB → 8 GiB `m7g` tier). Raw JSON: `docs/investigations/node_linux_{250k,50k,50k_cap}.json`. REPORT.md §Node (full landed tables + head-to-head + caveats) / TL;DR row / both status matrices / hardware row / commercial-reframe / consolidated caveats / top banner / change log all updated; `node/README.md` carries the results. Authoritative record is `bench/REPORT.md` — this entry is the network-runtime-tracker pointer.

  7. Flips line 136 to `[x]`. Likely composes with the existing kara-katas BENCH.md cadence — exact CI integration TBD when the slice lands.

- [x] **P0 — Flagship Demo 2: Parallax — COMPLETE 2026-06-06 (both close-out gates met).** Auto-concurrency proof — sends/receives(Network) routing through the event loop, fan-out joins, the differentiator at scale. **Close-out (v7 sweep, 2026-06-06):** gate (a) done (codegen gaps A+B fixed + `server.kara` weaves real `Dashboard` data into the body, byte-identical wire shape) and gate (b) PASSED — the five-impl single-session re-bench shows **Kāra clean vs the 2026-05-10 baseline** at `-c100` (req/s 720→738 +2.5%, p99 300→272 ms −9.4% i.e. *improved*, both inside ±10%), Go clean (req/s +3.0%, p99 −4.3%), Node unchanged (degenerate single-process). **Headline holds: Kāra `-c100` p99 2.6× lower than Rust** (272 vs 710), matching baseline's 2.68×.
